                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of African rice ( Oryza glaberrima ) and evidence for independent domestication"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2014 46:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-09-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mingsheng Chen, Klaus Mayer, Steve Rounsley, Rod Wing and colleagues report the genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima), a different species than Asian rice. The authors resequenced 20 O. glaberrima accessions and 94 Oryza barthii accessions (the putative progenitor species of O. glaberrima), and their analyses support the hypothesis that O. glaberrima was domesticated in a single region along the upper Niger river. The cultivation of rice in Africa dates back more than 3,000 years. Interestingly, African rice is not of the same origin as Asian rice (Oryza sativa L.) but rather is an entirely different species (i.e., Oryza glaberrima Steud.). Here we present a high-quality assembly and annotation of the O. glaberrima genome and detailed analyses of its evolutionary history of domestication and selection. Population genomics analyses of 20 O. glaberrima and 94 Oryza barthii accessions support the hypothesis that O. glaberrima was domesticated in a single region along the Niger river as opposed to noncentric domestication events across Africa. We detected evidence for artificial selection at a genome-wide scale, as well as with a set of O. glaberrima genes orthologous to O. sativa genes that are known to be associated with domestication, thus indicating convergent yet independent selection of a common set of genes during two geographically and culturally distinct domestication processes."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Mingsheng Chen, Klaus Mayer, Steve Rounsley, Rod Wing and colleagues report the genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima), a different species than Asian rice. The authors resequenced 20 O. glaberrima accessions and 94 Oryza barthii accessions (the putative progenitor species of O. glaberrima), and their analyses support the hypothesis that O. glaberrima was domesticated in a single region along the upper Niger river."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The cultivation of rice in Africa dates back more than 3,000 years. Interestingly, African rice is not of the same origin as Asian rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i> L.) but rather is an entirely different species (i.e., <i>Oryza glaberrima</i> Steud.). Here we present a high-quality assembly and annotation of the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome and detailed analyses of its evolutionary history of domestication and selection. Population genomics analyses of 20 <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 94 <i>Oryza barthii</i> accessions support the hypothesis that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated in a single region along the Niger river as opposed to noncentric domestication events across Africa. We detected evidence for artificial selection at a genome-wide scale, as well as with a set of <i>O. glaberrima</i> genes orthologous to <i>O. sativa</i> genes that are known to be associated with domestication, thus indicating convergent yet independent selection of a common set of genes during two geographically and culturally distinct domestication processes.</p></div></div></section>

    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>O. glaberrima</i> Steud. is an African species of rice that was independently domesticated from the wild progenitor <i>O. barthii</i> <span class="stix">∼</span>3,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sweeney, M. &amp; McCouch, S. The complex history of the domestication of rice. Ann. Bot. 100, 951–957 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d86e974">1</a></sup>, 6,000–7,000 years after the domestication of Asian rice (<i>O. sativa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Portères, R. in Origins of African Plant Domestication (ed. Harlan, J.R.) 409–452 (Mouton Publishers, 1976)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d86e981">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Vaughan, D.A., Lu, B.R. &amp; Tomooka, N. The evolving story of rice evolution. Plant Sci. 174, 394–408 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d86e984">3</a></sup>. Rice cultivation had a central role in building strong, knowledgeable and vibrant agrarian cultures in west Africa. It was this know-how, i.e., cultivation in lowland, upland and mangrove environments, harvesting and milling, that brought rice cultivation to the New World through slavery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Littlefield, D.C. Rice and Slaves: Ethnicity and the Slave Trade in Colonial South Carolina (University of Illinois Press, 1991)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d86e988">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Portères, R. in Papers in African Prehistory (eds. Fage, J.D. &amp; Oliver, R.A.) 43–58 (Cambridge University Press, 1970)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d86e991">5</a></sup>. <i>O. glaberrima</i>, likely the first rice cultivated in the New World, was probably introduced aboard Portuguese slave ships, but higher yielding Asian rice varieties (<i>O. sativa</i>) subsequently supplanted it<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Carney, J.A. Black Rice: the African Origins of Rice Cultivation in the Americas (Harvard University Press, 2001)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d86e1002">6</a></sup>.</p><p><i>O. glaberrima</i> is well adapted for cultivation in west Africa and possesses traits for increased tolerance to biotic and abiotic stresses, including drought, soil acidity, iron and aluminum toxicity, as well as weed competitiveness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brar, D.S. &amp; Khush, G.S. Alien introgression in rice. Plant Mol. Biol. 35, 35–47 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d86e1011">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez, P.L., Wing, R.A. &amp; Brar, D.S. in Genetics and Genomics of Rice (eds. Zhang, Q. &amp; Wing, R.A.) 9–25 (Springer, New York, 2013)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d86e1014">8</a></sup>. The <i>O. glaberrima</i> accession CG14, sequenced here, is one of the parents used in the generation of the 'New Rice for Africa' (NERICA) cultivars that revolutionized rice cultivation in west Africa by combining the high-yielding traits of Asian rice with the adaptive traits of west African rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Jones, M.P., Dingkuhn, M., Aluko, G.K. &amp; Semon, M. Interspecific Oryza sativa L. X O. glaberrima Steud. progenies in upland rice improvement. Euphytica 94, 237–246 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d86e1021">9</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we present a high-quality assembly and annotation of the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome and detailed population genomics analyses of its domestication history and selection.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We sequenced the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome (International Rice Germplasm Collection (IRGC) accession #96717, var. CG14) using a minimum tiling path (MTP) of 3,485 BAC clones selected from a BAC-based physical map aligned to the <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> reference genome (RefSeq)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d86e1052">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d86e1055">11</a></sup> with (i) a hybrid BAC pool (3,319 BACs) and whole genome shotgun approach using Roche/454 GS-FLX Titanium sequencing technology for 11.5 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Rounsley, S. et al. De novo next generation sequencing of plant genomes. Rice 2, 35–43 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d86e1059">12</a></sup> and (ii) a BAC-by-BAC (166 BACs) Sanger method for the short arm of chromosome 3 (Chr3S). The overall run statistics are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 1</a>. The genome was assembled as outlined in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Figure 1</a> and is composed of 5,309 scaffolds (scaffold N50, 217 kb) assembled into 12 pseudomolecules, resulting in a total assembly size of 316 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 2</a>). About 90% of the genome assembly (scaffold N50, 231 kb) could be ordered and oriented unambiguously based on the <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> RefSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d86e1079">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p>We evaluated the final assembly for accuracy and completeness using four previously Sanger-sequenced and finished BACs located on chromosomes 1 (1 BAC), 5 (1 BAC) and 6 (2 BACs). Overall, more than 98% of the query BAC sequences were detected and localized in the correct chromosomal locations (549,598 bp query/557,270 bp subject) with a sequence accuracy of 99.6%. The 7.7 kb of missing sequence was located across 23 sequence gaps in the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Protein coding and tRNA predictions</h3><p>The identification of protein coding gene models was based on consensus predictions derived from several types of evidence: <i>ab initio</i> gene finders, protein homology from finished plant genome projects and optimal spliced alignments of ESTs and tentative consensus transcripts. In addition to consensus models, we also searched for potentially missing candidate models using a Markov model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Parra, G., Blanco, E. &amp; Guigo, R. GeneID in Drosophila. Genome Res. 10, 511–515 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d86e1104">13</a></sup> that provides the likelihood for the coding potential of a given sequence. tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lowe, T.M. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d86e1108">14</a></sup> using default parameters.</p><p>In total, we derived 33,164 gene models, including potential gene models and 701 tRNA genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Tab1">Table 1</a> shows a comparison of the predicted protein coding and tRNA gene content of four sequenced <i>Oryza</i> species, including <i>O. glaberrima</i>. The gene number in <i>O. glaberrima</i> falls between that of <i>Oryza brachyantha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chen, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d86e1133">15</a></sup> (30,952 genes) and that of the gold standard, <i>O. sativa</i> RefSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d86e1140">11</a></sup> (41,620 genes).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The O. glaberrima genome (CG14 v1)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome (CG14 v1).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="658"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Concentric circles show structural, functional and evolutionary aspects of the genome: A, chromosome number; B, heat map view of genes; C, repeat (RNA and DNA TEs without MITEs) density in 200-kb windows (red, average +1 s.d.; blue, average −1 s.d.; yellow, gene and repeat density between red and blue); and D paralogous relationships between <i>O. glaberrima</i> chromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Gene feature statistics of four <i>Oryza</i> genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3044/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine the extent of conservation of functional genes in <i>O. glaberrima</i>, we selected a set of <i>O. sativa</i> genes from three important pathways associated with flowering time, light response and stress resistance (WRKY genes) and searched for orthologous genes in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome. We found that 95.5% (170/178) of the <i>O. sativa</i> genes tested were intact and syntenic within the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Tables 5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">9</a>).</p><p>To determine whether the eight missing genes were indeed not present in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome or were absent because of missing data or problems with the assembly, we scanned our baseline <i>O. glaberrima</i> RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) data set for evidence of transcription of these genes. The results (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>) demonstrate that seven of the eight genes are actually present and transcribed in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome but are located within or in close proximity to gaps in the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The last gene, <i>Hd1</i> (<i>heading date 1</i>), was shown previously to be deleted in <i>O. glaberrima</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sanyal, A. et al. Orthologous comparisons of the Hd1 region across genera reveal Hd1 gene lability within diploid Oryza species and disruptions to microsynteny in Sorghum. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 2487–2506 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d86e1584">16</a></sup>. Our assembly and RNA-Seq data support this finding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Repeat annotation and dynamics</h3><p>We found that transposable elements (TEs) represented 104 Mb (i.e., 34.25%) in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The largest classes of TEs are the long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) (16.65% total; Copia, 2.70%; Gypsy, 10.41%; unclassified elements, 3.55%), followed by DNA TEs (13.48% total, including miniature inverted-repeat TEs (MITEs)) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><p>MITE- and LTR-RT–related sequences represent 44.4% and 26.2% of the total number of TE sequences, respectively. The average distance between a TE-related sequence and the closest gene was 1,002 bp. Of the 28,249 predicted gene models located closer to TE sequences than to scaffold ends, <span class="stix">∼</span>63% of the TE-related sequences were MITEs and <span class="stix">∼</span>16% were LTR-RTs, so MITEs were enriched in their proximity to genes, whereas LTR-RTs were depleted. A similar trend holds true for the 16,136 predicted genes that include TE fragments: 9,685 insertions (60.02%) were MITEs and 2,703 (16.75%) were LTR-RT fragments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 12</a>).</p><p>The estimated divergence time between <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i> lineages, i.e., <span class="stix">∼</span>600,000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ammiraju, J.S.S. et al. Dynamic evolution of Oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d86e1623">17</a></sup>, provides a unique opportunity to study the dynamics of TE-driven structural changes through a genome-wide comparative approach. TEs constitute 104 Mb of the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome, and they represent 156 Mb of the <i>O. sativa</i> genome. The 52-Mb difference is consistent with the overall size difference between the two genomes, which shows that this difference could be accounted for solely by transposable elements. Moreover, given both the high synteny level and sequence identity between the two genomes, we conducted a thorough comparative survey of all TE insertions, thus distinguishing between insertions that occurred before (common in both genomes) and those that occurred after divergence from their common ancestor (either <i>O. sativa</i>– or <i>O. glaberrima</i>–specific insertions). When we considered Helitrons, short interspersed elements (SINEs) and LTR-RTs (both complete elements and solo LTRs), we found that 54.2% of the insertions occurred before their divergence, whereas 11.5% occurred in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 34.3% occurred in <i>O. sativa</i> after their divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Specifically, this finding confirms that recent transpositional activity has been greater in the <i>O. sativa</i> lineage. A closer examination of LTR-RT insertions showed that distinct families were active in each genome. For example, the Hopi family, amplified recently in the <i>O. sativa</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vitte, C., Panaud, O. &amp; Quesneville, H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics 8, 218 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d86e1656">18</a></sup>, has over 500 complete copies representing more than 5 Mb of sequence in <i>O. sativa</i>, whereas we found only one truncated copy in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome.</p><p>Our data demonstrate that TE-driven genome differentiation occurred on a short time scale between <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> through the activation of distinct families in each lineage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Domestication history of <i>O. glaberrima</i></h3><p>The geographical origins of crop domestication in Africa have been seen through two lenses—centric and noncentric<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Harlan, J.R. Agricultural origins: centers and noncenters. Science 174, 468–474 (1971)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d86e1686">19</a></sup>. Portères (1962)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Portéres, R. Berceaux agricoles primaires sur le continent Africain. J. Afr. Hist. 3, 195–210 (1962)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d86e1690">20</a></sup> first proposed that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated in the inland delta of the upper Niger river and diffused to two secondary centers of diversification, one along the Senegambian coast and the other in the interior Guinea highlands. Harlan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Harlan, J.R. Agricultural origins: centers and noncenters. Science 174, 468–474 (1971)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d86e1697">19</a></sup> proposed a noncentric model of domestication for most African plants (such as sorghum) but by 1976 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Harlan, J., De Wet, J. &amp; Stemler, A. in Origins of African Plant Domestication (ed. Harlan, J.R.) 3–20 (Mouton Publishers, 1976)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d86e1700">21</a>) agreed with Portères' hypothesis, treating <i>O. glaberrima</i> as an exception to this general pattern. Recent genetic analyses of 14 genes have supported Portères' hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, Z.M., Zheng, X.M. &amp; Ge, S. Genetic diversity and domestication history of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) as inferred from multiple gene sequences. Theor. Appl. Genet. 123, 21–31 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d86e1708">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Because <i>O. barthii</i> can be found <i>in situ</i> across most of the African continent, it is important to establish the population structure, if any, of a diverse collection of <i>O. barthii</i> accessions and to associate one or more of these populations with a collection of domesticated <i>O. glaberrima</i> varieties. To perform these experiments, we first resequenced 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions (threefold coverage each) from 12 African nations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 14</a>) and determined their population structure and genetic distance with respect to the CG14 <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome. A total of 8,174,678 <i>O. barthii</i> SNPs were identified and input into the maximum-likelihood clustering program ADMIXTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Alexander, D.H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d86e1740">23</a></sup> with <i>K</i> values ranging from two to six. ADMIXTURE partitioned the <i>O. barthii</i> accessions into four subgroups (OB-I, OB-II, OB-III and OB-V), as well as one apparent admixture subgroup (OB-IV) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 15</a>). This subgrouping was further supported by principal component analysis (PCA) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) and both neighbor-joining (NJ) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) and maximum likelihood trees (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) using the same SNP data set.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Population structure analysis of 94 O. barthii accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Population structure analysis of 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="434"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Population structure of 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions inferred using ADMIXTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Alexander, D.H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d86e1795">23</a></sup>. Each color represents one population. The length of each segment in each vertical bar represents the proportion contributed by ancestral populations. The <i>O. barthii</i> population is partitioned into four subgroups (OB-I, OB-II, OB-III and OB-V), as well as an admixed group (OB-IV). (<b>b</b>) PCA of 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions using all identified SNPs as markers. The <i>O. barthii</i> accessions from the same subgroup are clustered together. (<b>c</b>) NJ phylogenetic tree of 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It should be noted that <i>O. barthii</i> group III (OB-III) appears to be genetically distinct from the rest of the <i>O. barthii</i> accessions tested. Although the molecular and functional nature of this distinction has yet to be investigated, we do know that the majority of the accessions in OB-III were collected from Nigeria, and thus this pattern could be associated with local adaptation.</p><p>Next we resequenced 20 diverse <i>O. glaberrima</i> accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 16</a>) to threefold coverage, called SNPs relative to the CG14 genome assembly and constructed a NJ tree using the combined <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> SNP data. The NJ tree showed that that all but one <i>O. glaberrima</i> accession clustered with <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup OB-V (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). To measure population differences and similarities, we calculated the fixation index values (<i>F</i><sub><b>ST</b></sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Holsinger, K.E. &amp; Weir, B.S. Genetics in geographically structured populations: defining, estimating and interpreting FST. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 639–650 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d86e1869">24</a></sup> between each <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup and <i>O. glaberrima</i>. The pairwise <i>F</i><sub><b>ST</b></sub> value between OB-V and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (0.009) was smaller than the pairwise <i>F</i><sub><b>ST</b></sub> values between other subgroups and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (range, 0.055–0.369) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 17</a>). Thus, our data suggest that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated directly from <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup OB-V.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Identification of the domestication center of O. glaberrima."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Identification of the domestication center of <i>O. glaberrima</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="299"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) NJ phylogenetic tree of 20 <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 94 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions. All but one of the <i>O. glaberrima</i> accessions (black) are clustered with <i>O. barthii</i> accessions from group OB-V (green). (<b>b</b>) The proportion of each group of <i>O. barthii</i> accessions originating from different countries in Africa. All <i>O. barthii</i> accessions collected from the countries in the proposed domestication center (highlighted in black) are from the OB-V and OB-IV admixture groups. The proportion of <i>O. barthii</i> from the OB-V and OB-IV admixture groups found in each country decreased with distance from the domestication center, whereas the <i>O. barthii</i> accessions from other subgroups showed the opposite trend.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The combined population genetic analyses described above provide evidence that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated directly from the OB-V <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup and allowed us to estimate the approximate domestication center of <i>O. glaberrima</i> by calculating the proportion of each <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup originating from different geographical regions in Africa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 18</a>). All of the <i>O. barthii</i> accessions collected from the countries in the proposed domestication center (i.e., Gambia, Guinea, Senegal and Sierra Leone) are from the OB-V and OB-IV admixture groups, whereas the other sampled regions possess fewer and fewer OB-V accessions relative to their distance from the proposed domestication center (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). These results support the domestication theory proposed by Portères and are consistent with those reported by Li <i>et al</i>. (2011)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, Z.M., Zheng, X.M. &amp; Ge, S. Genetic diversity and domestication history of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) as inferred from multiple gene sequences. Theor. Appl. Genet. 123, 21–31 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d86e1992">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evidence of artificial selection in <i>O. glaberrima</i></h3><p>Results from the analyses described above indicate that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated from <i>O. barthii</i> in a single domestication center in west Africa. Studies of domestication in many plant species have demonstrated a reduction in genetic diversity in domesticated crops relative to their wild progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d86e2014">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053–1059 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d86e2017">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wright, S.I. et al. The effects of artificial selection on the maize genome. Science 308, 1310–1314 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d86e2020">27</a></sup>. To compare the genetic diversity present within African rice and its wild progenitor and identify signals of artificial selection during domestication, we first resequenced 20 <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 19 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Tables 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">19</a>) to a deeper coverage (10–30×) than that described in the previous section. A total of 4,447,424 and 8,735,082 SNPs were identified across the 20 <i>O. glaberrima</i> accessions and 19 <i>O. barthii</i> accessions, respectively, and were used to measure genome-wide levels of nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>), the minor allele frequency (MAF) spectrum, the decay of linkage disequilibrium (LD) and Tajima's <i>D</i> values across all annotated genes and intergenic regions. The levels of nucleotide variability in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 2.281) were significantly lower than those in <i>O. barthii</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 5.324) (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">9</a>). The levels of nucleotide variability of all the annotated genes in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 4.457) were significantly lower than those in <i>O. barthii</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 6.835) (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). The levels of nucleotide variability of intergenic regions in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 4.799) were also significantly lower than those in <i>O. barthii</i> (<i>π</i> per kb, 8.506) (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). The MAF spectrum of <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> detected an excess of low-frequency alleles in <i>O. glaberrima</i> compared with <i>O. barthii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Tajima's <i>D</i> values in all annotated genes were significantly more negative in <i>O. glaberrima</i> than in <i>O. barthii</i> (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>). Furthermore, LD decay analysis revealed that LD decay blocks were four times larger in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (251 kb) than in <i>O. barthii</i> (57 kb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). These combined results are strongly suggestive of a significant reduction in genetic diversity in <i>O. glaberrima</i> as a consequence of domestication.</p><p>To identify genomic regions under artificial selection in <i>O. glaberrima</i>, we calculated the nucleotide diversity ratio (<i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub>) between <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup OB-V (wild rice) and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (cultivated rice) using 100-kb windows<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d86e2180">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105–111 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d86e2183">28</a></sup>. A total of 73 regions were identified in the top 2.5 percentile of genetic diversity cutoffs (<i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub> &gt; 5.8) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 20</a>), one of which contains an ortholog of the <i>O. sativa</i> domestication-related gene <i>badh2</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kovach, M.J., Calingacion, M.N., Fitzgerald, M.A. &amp; McCouch, S.R. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14444–14449 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d86e2207">29</a>), which is associated with fragrance in Asian rice.</p><p>To detect evidence of recent selective sweeps in the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome, we used the SNP data set described above to search for site frequency spectrum (SFS) deviations relative to genome-wide patterns using the composite likelihood ratio statistic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nielsen, R. et al. Genomic scans for selective sweeps using SNP data. Genome Res. 15, 1566–1575 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d86e2217">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). We conducted the same analyses in the <i>O. barthii</i> subgroup OB-V to identify regions that only showed evidence of selective sweeps in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Scanning the genome in 3-kb windows, we identified the top 0.5% outlier regions and first searched for regions close to or encompassing homologs of known and hypothesized <i>O. sativa</i> domestication genes. Homologs of seven agronomically important <i>O. sativa</i> genes with diverse functions (<i>Sd1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Spielmeyer, W., Ellis, M.H. &amp; Chandler, P.M. Semidwarf (sd-1), “green revolution” rice, contains a defective gibberellin 20-oxidase gene. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 9043–9048 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d86e2242">31</a>), <i>OsNAC6</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nakashima, K. et al. Functional analysis of a NAC-type transcription factor OsNAC6 involved in abiotic and biotic stress-responsive gene expression in rice. Plant J. 51, 617–630 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d86e2249">32</a>), <i>EP3</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Piao, R. et al. Map-based cloning of the ERECT PANICLE 3 gene in rice. Theor. Appl. Genet. 119, 1497–1506 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d86e2255">33</a>), <i>Sh4</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, C., Zhou, A. &amp; Sang, T. Rice domestication by reducing shattering. Science 311, 1936–1939 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d86e2261">34</a>), <i>Ep2</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhu, K. et al. ERECT PANICLE2 encodes a novel protein that regulates panicle erectness in indica rice. Genetics 184, 343–350 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d86e2268">35</a>), <i>Sub1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu, K. et al. Sub1A is an ethylene-response-factor-like gene that confers submergence tolerance to rice. Nature 442, 705–708 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d86e2274">36</a>) and <i>Xa26</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sun, X. et al. Xa26, a gene conferring resistance to Xanthomonas oryzae pv. oryzae in rice, encodes an LRR receptor kinase-like protein. Plant J. 37, 517–527 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d86e2280">37</a>)) are close—within the window of LD decay—to regions with strong evidence of recent selective sweeps in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 21</a>). In addition, we conducted a genome scan of <i>O. glaberrima</i> for regions of high haplotype homozygosity using the integrated haplotype score (iHS) statistic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Voight, B.F., Kudaravalli, S., Wen, X. &amp; Pritchard, J.K. A map of recent positive selection in the human genome. PLoS Biol. 4, e72 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d86e2294">38</a></sup>. These analyses identified several regions with unusually long haplotypes relative to the whole genome, suggesting recent incomplete selective sweeps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">15</a>). We examined the top 500 SNPs with the largest |iHS| (|iHS| &gt; 4, corresponding to 0.028% of the analyzed SNPs genome wide) and identified 24 candidate regions of multiple contiguous SNPs with |iHS| &gt; 4. Three of those regions were close (8–190 kb)—within the window of LD decay—to genes previously identified to be under domestication in Asian rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 22</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Identification of candidate regions of artificial selection during O. glaberrima domestication."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Identification of candidate regions of artificial selection during <i>O. glaberrima</i> domestication.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="596"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Plot of the composite likelihood ratio (CLR) across <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome. The red dashed line indicates the cutoff value for the 0.5% outlier regions with significant deviations from neutrality, indicating evidence of recent selective sweeps.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The regions identified with signals of artificial selection from all the tests described above can be considered candidate regions for future genetic and functional analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Evidence for independent domestication of <i>O. glaberrima</i></h3><p>Although the domestication process of Asian rice has been widely investigated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d86e2348">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Molina, J. et al. Molecular evidence for a single evolutionary origin of domesticated rice. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 8351–8356 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d86e2351">39</a></sup>, it remains unclear whether the domestication of <i>O. glaberrima</i> underwent similar or independent paths. That is, did ancient Asian and African farmers artificially select, in parallel, the same or similar traits and genes during the domestication process, did they select a completely different set of traits and genes, or did they use a combination of both processes? Two recent studies have suggested that both scenarios could be in play. First, Gross <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gross, B.L., Steffen, F.T. &amp; Olsen, K.M. The molecular basis of white pericarps in African domesticated rice: novel mutations at the Rc gene. J. Evol. Biol. 23, 2747–2753 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d86e2361">40</a></sup> showed that <i>Rc</i>, the red pericarp gene, has signatures of selection in both Asian and African rice but that the genetic diversity profile of <i>Rc</i> is different and independent for each species, which is suggestive of convergent but independent domestication. As described above, <i>Hd1</i>, a key gene in Asian rice that controls synchronized flowering, is completely deleted in <i>O. glaberrima</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sanyal, A. et al. Orthologous comparisons of the Hd1 region across genera reveal Hd1 gene lability within diploid Oryza species and disruptions to microsynteny in Sorghum. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 2487–2506 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d86e2377">16</a></sup>. However, this gene is intact and expressed in <i>O. barthii</i> (IRGC accession #105608; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), further supporting the hypothesis of independent domestication of a common trait but by the selection of a different set of gene(s) and/or quantitative trait loci.</p><p>To make the comparative investigation of domestication in Asian and African rice even more complex, there is evidence that the domestication of African rice in more recent times may have been influenced by the introduction of Asian rice into west Africa and subsequent intercrossing, as suggested by the microsatellite study of Semon <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Semon, M., Nielsen, R., Jones, M.P. &amp; McCouch, S.R. The population structure of African cultivated rice Oryza glaberrima (Steud.): evidence for elevated levels of linkage disequilibrium caused by admixture with O. sativa and ecological adaptation. Genetics 169, 1639–1647 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d86e2393">41</a></sup> that inferred substantial admixture between some accessions of both species. To address this latter concern and look for evidence of introgression of Asian rice alleles into African rice, we first probed the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome assembly with resequencing reads of 14 <i>O. sativa</i> (7 <i>O. sativa</i> spp. <i>japonica</i> and 7 <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 23</a>) and 20 <i>O. glaberrima</i> accessions. We then used a newly developed haplotype-based summary statistic that compares the minimum number of nucleotide differences between sequences from two different species to the average number of between-species differences to identify regions of the genome that have experienced recent introgression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Geneva, A.J., Lovato, L.M., Kingan, S.B. &amp; Garrigan, D. A new method to scan genomes for introgression in a secondary contact model. 
http://arxiv.org/abs/1403.1552

 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d86e2422">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Garrigan, D. POPBAM: tools for evolutionary analysis of short read sequence alignments. Evol. Bioinform. Online 9, 343–353 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d86e2425">43</a></sup>. Our genome-wide analyses were unable to detect any evidence of recent introgression between the sequenced accessions of the two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>), thus indicating that <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> were domesticated independently.</p><p>As we detected no evidence of recent introgression of <i>O. sativa</i> into <i>O. glaberrima</i>, we next investigated whether a known set of <i>O. sativa</i> domestication genes was also artificially selected during the domestication of African rice. We used 19 <i>O. sativa</i> domestication genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 24</a>) to probe the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome, resulting in the identification of 16 clear orthologous loci. Three of these 16 genes (<i>qSh1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Konishi, S., et al. An SNP caused loss of seed shattering during rice domestication. Science 312, 1392–1396 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d86e2463">44</a>), <i>Sd1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Spielmeyer, W., Ellis, M.H. &amp; Chandler, P.M. Semidwarf (sd-1), “green revolution” rice, contains a defective gibberellin 20-oxidase gene. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 9043–9048 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d86e2469">31</a>) and <i>Dep1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Huang, X. et al. Natural variation at the DEP1 locus enhances grain yield in rice. Nat. Genet. 41, 494–497 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d86e2476">45</a>)) yielded substantial deviations from neutral expectations in Tajima's <i>D</i> tests (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 25</a>). These results were further supported by comparing the nucleotide diversity of these genes in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> to the genome-wide nucleotide diversity of intergenic regions in both species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Tables 26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">27</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">28</a>). In addition, the nucleotide diversity of these genes was greatly reduced in <i>O. glaberrima</i> relative to <i>O. barthii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">19</a>). These data suggest that a minimum of four genes, including <i>Rc</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gross, B.L., Steffen, F.T. &amp; Olsen, K.M. The molecular basis of white pericarps in African domesticated rice: novel mutations at the Rc gene. J. Evol. Biol. 23, 2747–2753 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d86e2520">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Sweeney, M.T., Thomson, M.J., Pfeil, B.E. &amp; McCouch, S. Caught red-handed: Rc encodes a basic helix-loop-helix protein conditioning red pericarp in rice. Plant Cell 18, 283–294 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d86e2523">46</a></sup>, that are associated with domestication in <i>O. sativa</i> may have been under recent selection in <i>O. glaberrima</i>.</p><p>The selection of non-shattering panicles has been associated with crop domestication for a wide variety of crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Doebley, J.F., Gaut, B.S. &amp; Smith, B.D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell 127, 1309–1321 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d86e2536">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Meyer, R.S. &amp; Purugganan, M.D. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 840–852 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d86e2539">48</a></sup>. Because orthologs of a gene that controls shattering in <i>O. sativa</i> (<i>qSh1</i>) were shown to possess signals of artificial selection in <i>O. glaberrima</i>, we investigated the molecular structure and transcriptional nature of this gene and two additional shattering gene orthologs from <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Shattering 1</i> and <i>Shattering 4</i>, to search for evidence of independent domestication.</p><p>The <i>O. sativa Shattering 1</i> gene (<i>OsSh1</i>: LOC_Os03g44710) encodes a YABBY transcription factor whose gene has an <span class="stix">∼</span>4-kb insertion in its third intron, thus leading to reduced transcription and a shattering-resistant phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i> compared to <i>Oryza rufipogon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Lin, Z. et al. Parallel domestication of the Shattering1 genes in cereals. Nat. Genet. 44, 720–724 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d86e2577">49</a></sup>. Functional orthologs of this gene have also been identified in sorghum and maize, confirming the independent domestication of shattering genes in cereals in general. Sequence analysis of the orthologous region in <i>O. glaberrima</i> revealed the presence of a 45-kb deletion that resulted in the complete ablation of the <i>O. glaberrima OsSh1</i> ortholog and three additional genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). We confirmed the presence of this gene in the wild progenitor, and its absence in the domesticated species, by sequence analysis of BACs encompassing this orthologous region from <i>O. barthii</i> (IRGC accession #105608) and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (CG14) and RNA-Seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Sequence comparisons of OsSh1 and Sh4."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Sequence comparisons of <i>OsSh1</i> and <i>Sh4</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3044/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3044_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="488"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Orthologous gene relationship of the <i>OsSh1</i> region of <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> (Os) with those of <i>O. barthii</i> (Ob) and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (Og). The 45-kb deletion (red triangle) resulted in the complete removal of the <i>OsSh1</i> ortholog and three additional genes in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (red rectangle). Inversion is indicated with blue arrows. U and D represent upstream and downstream genes relative to <i>OsSh1</i>, respectively (U1, LOC_Os03g44680; U2, LOC_Os03g44690; <i>OsSh1</i>, LOC_Os03g44710; D1, LOC_Os03g44720; D2, LOC_Os03g44740; D3, LOC_Os03g44750; D4, LOC_Os03g44760; D5, LOC_Os03g44780). (<b>b</b>) Sequence comparison of <i>Sh4</i> of <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i>. Two insertions (Ins) and three deletions (Del) of <i>O. glaberrima</i> compared to <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> are shown as brown triangles and blue triangles, respectively. Ten SNPs are labeled a–j. The causative mutation (f) of the non-shattering phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i> is highlighted in red. This mutation did not exist in <i>O. glaberrima</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3044/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>O. sativa Shattering 4</i> gene (<i>Sh4</i>: LOC_Os04g57530) encodes a Myb3 transcription factor in which a single-nucleotide substitution leads to reduced transcription and a shattering-resistant phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i> compared to <i>O. nivara</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, C., Zhou, A. &amp; Sang, T. Rice domestication by reducing shattering. Science 311, 1936–1939 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d86e2708">34</a></sup>. Sequence analysis of the orthologous genic region in <i>O. glaberrima</i> revealed a completely different set of sequence variation relative to the <i>O. sativa</i> gene that includes the presence of ten SNPs and five small insertion/deletions. Most notably, the causative mutation revealing the non-shattering phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i> was not found in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). Analysis of <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> RNA-Seq expression data from mixed-stage panicles from both <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> showed a differential expression pattern with no transcription detected in <i>O. glaberrima</i>, whereas transcription was detected in <i>O. barthii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). As the expression level of <i>OgSh4</i> is absent or reduced in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and this gene is located close to a region under a selective sweep, it is possible that the causative mutation may reside in the upstream promoter region. Thus, we searched the promoter regions of <i>OgSh4</i> in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> for signatures of selection and found that the promoter region of <i>O. glaberrima</i> shows substantial deviation from neutral expectations using Tajima's <i>D</i> test (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 25</a>). The result was further supported by comparing the nucleotide diversity of the promoter region in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> to the genome-wide nucleotide diversity of intergenic regions in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 29</a>). In addition, the nucleotide diversity of the promoter region was greatly reduced in <i>O. glaberrima</i> relative to <i>O. barthii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). This result suggests that the promoter region of <i>OgSh4</i> may have been selected during domestication, leading to reduction of the expression of <i>OgSh4</i>.</p><p>The <i>O. sativa qSh1</i> (LOC_Os01g62920) locus encodes a transcription factor that has a single SNP located 12 kb upstream of the transcription start site. This mutation prevents transcription in the abscission layer that results in a shattering-resistant phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica</i> compared with <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Konishi, S., et al. An SNP caused loss of seed shattering during rice domestication. Science 312, 1392–1396 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d86e2824">44</a></sup>. Sequence comparison of the <i>O. sativa qSh1</i> locus and its orthologous locus in <i>O. glaberrima</i> showed a high level of sequence identity but did not reveal the presence of the causal <i>O. sativa</i> SNP that leads to the shattering phenotype in <i>O. sativa</i>. RNA-Seq analysis of the <i>qSh1</i> ortholog showed that the gene was transcribed in both <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> panicles and presumably functions normally in comparison to the gene expression pattern observed in Asian rice subspecies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>).</p><p>The comparative analysis described above provides persuasive evidence that ancient African and Asian farmers unknowingly selected for mutations in all three orthologous genes investigated that prevented or reduced panicle shattering. We also demonstrate that the mutation profiles of each orthologous pair of these genes (<i>OsSh1</i>, <i>Sh4</i> and <i>qSh1</i>) are completely different. Thus, we conclude that the control of panicle shattering in African and Asian rice is the result of independent domestication. Combined with the results of genome-wide introgression analysis of <i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, we provide strong evidence that <i>O. glaberrima</i> was domesticated independently from <i>O. barthii</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>As the world population is projected to increase from 7.1 billion to over 9 billion by 2050, plant biologists must forge a second green revolution with the creation of crops that have two to three times the current yield with reduced inputs (i.e., less water, fertilizers and pesticides and the ability to grow on marginal soils)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhang, Q. Strategies for developing Green Super Rice. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 16402–16409 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d86e2888">50</a></sup>. Rice will have a key role in helping to solve the problem of how to feed 9 billion people<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Godfray, H.C. et al. Food security: the challenge of feeding 9 billion people. Science 327, 812–818 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3044#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d86e2892">51</a></sup>.</p><p>The release of the <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome, its annotation and comparative population genomics data sets enables an unprecedented opportunity for the identification and utilization of adaptive traits that are important for rice agriculture, especially in west Africa, a region whose population is expected to grow rapidly over the next 50 years.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11"><i>O. glaberrima</i> genome sequencing.</h3><p>A total of 3,485 MTP BAC clones were selected from the <i>O. glaberrima</i> BAC-based physical map. Of those, 166 BACs representing Chr3S were individually Sanger sequenced, and the remaining BACs were divided into 115 chromosome-specific BAC pools (28–30 chromosomally ordered BACs per pool). All MTP BACs were re–end sequenced and compared to the <i>O. glaberrima</i> BAC-end data set to validate the clone identity. About 3–5 μg of pooled BAC DNA was used to construct each Roche/454 Titanium library with multiplex identifiers (MID) using the manufacturer's protocol, followed by Bioanalyzer and fluorometry analyses to assess library quality and quantity, respectively. Clonal amplification of individual libraries was performed by emulsion-based clonal amplification (emPCR amplification), and a group of five to six MID-tagged DNA beads was pooled and loaded onto one picotiter plate (PTP) and sequenced on a Roche/454 GS FLX instrument using Titanium chemistry following the manufacturer's protocols. Chromosome-specific libraries (3-kb paired ends (PEs)) were constructed from the BAC pools and sequenced on one half PTP per chromosome. Additionally, about 5× genome–equivalent Titanium reads were generated using a whole genome shotgun (WGS) method. Overall sequencing statistics are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12"><i>O. glaberrima</i> genome assembly.</h3><p>The <i>O. glaberrima</i> genome was assembled using <span class="stix">∼</span>12.4 Gb of high-quality bases with the Newbler V2.3 assembler following a six-step protocol (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3044#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).
